[^0-1-24]: Robert and Helen Lynd, Middletown, op. cit., p. 350.

[^0-1-25]: Robert and Helen Lynd, Middletown in Transition, op. cit., p. 318.

[^0-1-26]: Ibid., p. 311.

[^0-1-27]: Weber, op. cit., pp. 181-183. See also Weber, *General Economic History* (New York, Collier Books, 1961), p. 270. Note that Weber's perceptiveness here makes it difficult to use Gastonia as a test for his more celebrated thesis. Is is possible, of course, to regard Gastonia as a representative of early stages of industrialization, in which case the captive status of Gastonia's religion both refines and rebutts Weber. But it is also possible to see Gastonia as occurring in the context of industrialization's highly developed stage. In this case, Weber himself would have predicted that religion would lack prime influence and that management would attain self-generating autonomy through the legitimation of economic values alone. While we suggest generally that Gastonia has more in common with the nineteenth century than the 1960's from the standpoint of its undifferentiated localism and its paternalistic industry, this is not to suggest that it has still more in common with the seventeenth century, and the gap between the seventeenth and the nineteenth century was important to Weber. Moreover, even if Gastonia were a throwback to the Reformation, it is important to see it in the ambience of a total society which is not anachronistic. Thus, economic values have taken on a dominance and a legitimacy in the society as a whole, and this should permeate Gastonia despite its attempts to maintain localism.
`韦伯，同上，第181-183页。另见韦伯，《世界经济史》（纽约：科利尔出版社，1961年），第270页。需注意，韦伯此处的敏锐洞察力使得将加斯顿尼亚作为检验其更著名论点的案例变得困难。当然，我们也可以将加斯顿尼亚视为工业化早期阶段的代表，如此一来，其宗教的依附性既细化了韦伯的论点，也对其构成反驳。但同样可能的是，将加斯顿尼亚置于工业化高度发展阶段的背景下考察。在这种情况下，韦伯本人本会预测宗教将不再具有首要影响力，而管理层将仅通过经济价值本身的正当性获得自我生成的自主性。尽管我们总体上认为，从其未分化的地域主义和家长式工业特征来看，加斯顿尼亚更接近十九世纪而非六十年代，但这并不意味着它与十七世纪更为相似——而十七世纪与十九世纪之间的差距对韦伯而言至关重要。此外，即使加斯顿尼亚在某种程度上是宗教改革时期的回响，也必须将其置于一个整体社会氛围中加以理解，而这一社会并非不合时宜。因此，经济价值在整个社会中已获得主导地位和正当性，这种影响应渗透至加斯顿尼亚，尽管它试图维持自身的地域主义。`

---

## Further Conditions for Religious Influence and Innovation in Secular Issues

Pope was centrally concerned with the problems of religious influence in secular affairs. His findings in Gastonia shocked a generation of clerics and helped to stimulate a re-evaluation of the church's posture. Not only did he see the churches as captives of the mills but he also brilliantly depicted the position of the religious "sect," whose distinctive attribute is withdrawal from secular issues in general. It would seem as if the change from sect to church is merely the change from passivity to prostitution.

And yet for all of the history that falls into this pattern, such a deterministic interpretation is too harsh, as Pope himself was aware. We have already mentioned structural differentiation as one process that has changed the church's position. Now we want to explore several additional factors that may produce wide variation even under conditions of differentiation itself. Since there is little to be added to Pope's discussion of the sects, we will restrict ourselves to churches. The discussion will be couched within five propositions. The first of these deals with an important contingency if the church is to have any influence at all on its parishioners. The next four deal with some conditions under which the church and the local minister are likely to oppose parishioner sentiment in the pursuit of doctrine or the clergyman's personal convictions. In general, we will see that Gastonia offered the worst of all possible worlds for religious initiative. Once again, we must be careful to avoid prejudging the world on the basis of Gastonia alone.

1. Church influence will depend upon the nature of parishioner involvement. Up to a point, of course, a minister can say anything at all, but his impact will depend upon the extent to which his flock is listening. It is quite possible to have an eloquent minister who is completely ineffective. It is equally possible to have a minister who stutters in the pulpit but whose every difficult word is followed. Now one of the consequences of

Pope's emphasis on the minister rather than the parishioner and on the institution rather than the individual is that the issue of involvement is unattended. Can we be sure that Gastonia's churches played an important role in creating a docile labor force when we do not know how deeply involved the labor force was in Gastonia's churches?

Actually there is reason to suspect that a good proportion of the millhands regarded the churches as unrelated to their work-a-day world. Pope concedes that religious participation had dropped off in Gastonia during the 1920's, perhaps because of the churches' collusion with management. Other studies indicate that, in general, the proportion of the working class who are church-involved is much smaller than is suggested by Marx's reference to religion as a mass opiate. Warner indicates that in Yankee City almost 70 per cent of the workers in the shoe factories had no religious preference[28] — a suspiciously large figure that is nevertheless provocative. Vidich and Bensman suggest that only 400 out of 1,700 town residents were active church participants and find that most of these were concentrated among the middle classes.[29] Underwood notes that token religious affiliation is more important than actual participation among workers in Holyoke.[30] Finally, Morland's study of a town much like Gastonia may provide a crucial distinction. He notes that the textile workers in "Kent" were extremely well-versed in a literal interpretation of the Bible, but little interested in the church as an institution.[31]

In all of this, it is possible to divide the working class into four groups and to speculate on their proportions in Gastonia. First, there are those who neither participate nor believe in religion in any form; these were clearly a small fraction in Gastonia, for it was precisely this allegation that contributed heavily to the discrediting of the union organizers among the millhands. Second, there are those who have a high degree of

28. Warner and Low, The Social System of the Modern Factory, op. cit., p. 219. Unhappily, while the figure arouses suspicion as too high, the authors do not present sufficient methodological detail to determine its contingencies.

29. Vidich and Bensman, op. cit., especially pp. 231-262: Part IV, "The Reconciliation of Symbolic Appearances and Institutional Realities."

30. Underwood, op. cit., especially Chapters 15 and 16.

31. Morland, op. cit., especially pp. 105 ff.

religious feeling and belief but who are alienated from the churches; as Morland indicates, these may have constituted a high percentage of the millhands. Third, there are those who participate without believing strongly; this should be a low percentage of the millhands since it is more of a middle-class characteristic and is hardly associated with the Southern fundamentalist Bible belt. Fourth, there are those who both participate and believe; these should also have formed a substantial percentage of the millworkers, though not necessarily a majority. In all of this, speculation is a poor substitute for data. Yet the question of involvement is an important intervening variable in tracing the connection between what the religious institution says and what the community does. Liberal and conservative ministers alike bemoan their lack of impact among the "Christmas and Easter attenders." They are also frequently surprised to find that their most "devout" parishioners are the least responsive to the church's organizational policies and requests.[32]

2. The church's autonomy from community and parishioner sentiment depends upon the structure of the denomination. If the church-sect distinction has become a leitmotiv of the sociology of religion, there is another, threefold distinction that has cut a swath through the study of American Protestantism. Here the differences concern the degree of bureaucratization and autonomy. According to the Congregational (or Baptist) model, each parish has complete local autonomy to choose its own pastor, its own ideology, and its own destiny. Under the Presbyterian (or Methodist) model, authority is concentrated in regional bodies. Under the Episcopalian (or Lutheran) scheme, there is a national hierarchy culminating in an archbishop and resembling the Catholic and Anglican structures from which it is derived. Like church and sect, these are ideal types, which means that they are often blurred in reality. As examples, Paul Harrison has demonstrated that bureaucratization has made significant inroads among the Northern Baptists despite their protestations of local autonomy;[33] the Episcopalians often respect the desires of a congregation, partly because its typically high-status parishioners are often especially powerful people in their own spheres. Despite these departures, however, the distinction helps to shed light on the problem of religious innovation and influence.

In general, the greater the centralization of denominational power and the greater the amount of impersonal bureaucracy, the greater the degree to which the minister can (and often must) hold out against the opposing views of his adherents. It is not surprising that the Episcopalians and the Lutherans were least adaptable to the Gastonia scene. In both churches, those in higher positions of denominational authority refused to allow a local capitulation to the segregation of social classes. Moreover, because of the financial support of the larger denomination, the local parishes were less vulnerable to local money. Thus the top levels of the hierarchy militated against compromises that were necessary for success at the parish level. On the other hand, the Baptists were adaptable indeed. Since the pastor is responsible only to his parishioners, he is free to conform to their needs. Put another way, since the lay church members hold the reins of power, the pastor is inhibited in advocating any opposing views or sentiments. Turning to Gastonia's Presbyterians and Methodists, both these denominations have distinct Southern branches that are historically separate within the over-all denominational structure. While the Presbyterians and the Methodists lacked the local autonomy of the Baptists they were able to avoid the national control of the Episcopalians and the Lutherans. They were in a better position to conform to the demands of class segregation and were also less fettered in making their peace with mill management.

Further confirmation of the importance of these differences comes from the more recent racial crisis in the South. It is predictable, for example, that the most racially liberal religious group in the South is the highly bureaucratic Catholic Church, given the expectation that its local parishes will conform to national policy and given the job security that the bureaucracy

affords the priests. In several cases, extreme segregationists have been excommunicated; in Alabama, the Catholic parochial schools now have more than the token integration that characterizes the public schools. It is also predictable that the Episcopalians should be among the most integrationist of the Protestant denominations. Not only are the local pastors given denominational support but the pastors are also brought into more sustained contact with their confreres from other areas of the country where racial liberalism is the expectation rather than the exception. Thus the pastor is given more organizational support and is more influenced by his liberal colleagues' expectations.

Much the same thing can be seen in the response of the churches to labor at the end of the nineteenth century. Although the Episcopalians had perhaps the highest percentage of management among their parishioners, they were responsive to the Social Gospel and to a new understanding, if not espousal, of the rights of labor. By contrast denominations like the Baptists were more recalcitrant. It was perhaps too much to expect their ministers to "go it alone." This was especially the case where, as in Gastonia, the opposing side of management was often responsible for the pastor's salary even though none of management actually belonged to his parish.

3. Religious innovation depends upon the level of the hierarchy from which a statement is issued. Part of this is simply a corollary of the previous proposition. The more bureaucratic the denomination, the more likely it is to risk departures from parishioner sentiment. And yet whereas the preceding was concerned only with statements from the local minister, here we are concerned with positions at all ranks in the hierarchy. In general, the higher the rank the more adventuresome the policy, since the higher the rank the more removed it is from the laity in general.

It is interesting to contrast Pope's work at the local level in Gastonia with Arthur James Todd's general appraisal of religion and industry, published in 1933.[34] Whereas Pope notes the capitulation of the churches to management, Todd points

to their noble resistance in the cause of labor. He cites statement after statement from the very denominations that Pope's analysis would seem to condemn. On the face of it, the two studies seem to be in sharp disagreement. And yet note that Todd is only concerned with general policies issued from denominational headquarters, which are separated by several rungs and a great deal of proverbial tape from the parishioners themselves. Pope himself mentions that in several denominations, statewide councils had produced statements deploring the Gastonia mill conditions and supporting the millhands. But as Pope goes on to indicate, it is one thing to issue broad pronouncements from the isolated heights of a clerical committee, and it is quite another to issue them in the local pulpit where the minister must stand alone against a belligerent congregation. Often the pastor has only the alternative of compromising his position to maintain the membership or maintaining his position while talking to an empty hall—if he is permitted to talk at all. In general, the former choice is preferred both because of pressures to keep the organization going and because of the hope that some of the "Christian spirit" will be communicated even if specific issues are skirted.

4. The church's position will depend upon the type of minister even within a single denomination. Certainly the age and status background of the minister, the kind of training he has received, the type of pulpit to which he is assigned, and whether or not his ordination conferred tenure will all have an impact on his stand. In general, younger ministers tend to be more independent and more adventuresome than their older colleagues. Those who come from seminaries connected with the better universities tend to be more liberal than those from the old-style "Bible Institutes." The higher the minister's own background status the more independent he is likely to be; conversely, the lower his status, the more he may seek compensatory approval from the high-status and often conservative members of his congregation. Finally, those with tenure tend, of course, to be more adventuresome than those whose job anxieties are less assuaged.

Pope is clear to the point of brilliance on the difficulties faced by the sophisticated clergymen confronting a provincial parish.

This was a prime reason why the Lutherans and the Presbyterians lost out in the battle for the millhands' religious allegiance and why the less sophisticated Methodists and Baptists won. It is worth noting, however, that even Methodists and Baptists have now begun to take the road to middle-class respectability. The Southern Methodists have already begun to complain about having a too narrowly upper middle-class clientele, marking a dramatic mobility relative to Gastonia. Recently Southern politicians were shocked by the reasoned argument of the Southern Baptist statement against the proposed amendment to the Constitution to allow school prayers. In the latter instance, however, it is important to remember that this was a statement from denominational headquarters and not from any local pulpit.

Moving out of Gastonia and into New York State, similar evidence of the plight of the young minister is given in Vidich and Bensmen's Small Town in Mass Society. The young minister who comes prepared to remake the town is faced by two hurdles. First, he must convince his congregation that his position is correct, which is not easy since his congregation will represent a good many conflicting views and unanimity on any issue is difficult to achieve. But even if he succeeds, there is still another obstacle in convincing the town as a whole. Not only is the town more diverse but there tends to be an unstated agreement among the local clergy that parish boundaries will be honored; informal persuasion of another pastor's membership is frowned upon. As a result, the young minister soon learns to swallow his principles and pigeon-hole the edicts of his denominational superiors. He is acculturated into the community and he finds himself re-made in its image rather than the reverse.

5. Religious innovation depends upon the type of issue and the degree of parishioner sentiment concerning it. One of the most provocative studies of church policy formation in recent years is Glock and Ringer's analysis of the Episcopalians.[35] Although the study focuses on policy decisions at a national convention, the laity were still very much involved, since under the Episcopalian system local pastors and selected lay representatives form one part of a bicameral scheme with the House of Bishops comprising the other. Glock and Ringer conclude that the religious leaders are most likely to takc innovative positions on issues that are removed from the immediate interests of the laity. They are least likely to be innovative in areas where the laity have strong feelings and immediate self-interest. The church may very well declare support for the recognition of Red China and the further pursuit of disarmament, since these are only distantly related to day-to-day concerns of the laity. The church may even declare strong support for civil rights, since the bulk of the lay representatives are from the North and will agree in the abstract. On the other hand, the church is much more inhibited on economic issues, particularly those that infringe upon the profits of Episcopalian employers through higher wages and higher taxes.

[^0-1-35]: Charles Y. Glock and Benjamin B. Ringer, “Church Policy and the Attitudes of Ministers and Parishioners on Social Issues,” American Sociological Review, 21 (April, 1956), 148-156.

Much of this applies to the dilemma of the local pastor as well. He, like his superiors in the national structure, must calculate his positions in terms of whom they are likely to alienate and whom they are likely to attract.[36] It is not unusual to find an extremely liberal position in one area as compensation for an embarrassed silence or a rhetorical side-step in another. Indeed some cynics have suggested that the recent burst of Northern clerical activity in support of civil rights is itself compensation for the inability to promote less celebrated causes in an upper middle-class pulpit.

We have tried, then, to specify five additional variables that affect the position of the church on nonreligious issues. While part of this is speculation, much of it is corroborated by previous studies in the literature and a good deal of it is supported by Pope himself. There is a very real sense in which Gastonia presented a unique conspiracy of factors militating against religious innovation. Not only were many of its groups more sectlike than churchlike but, in addition, a decline in religious involvement caused the local ministers to tread softly for fear of alienating the remainder. This fear was perhaps particularly pronounced for the higher-status laity, who are especially important to a church by dint of their greater financial contributions and their greater organizational know-how. Other elements of the conspiracy, however, include the fact that the largest denomination, the Baptist, was based on local autonomy and thus the minister was without support from a national headquarters. The issue was decidedly economic in a time when economics was the most salient issue of the day. And, finally, neither structural differentiation nor the mass society had developed to the point where religion was freed from its local ties and given the measure of independence necessary to issue more courageous, though possibly less influential statements.

36. As we shall indicate later, this point has been well illustrated with respect to the Southern racial crisis. See Ernest Q. Campbell and Thomas F. Pettigrew, Christians in Racial Crisis: A Study of Little Rock's Ministry (Washington, D.C., Public Affairs Press, 1959).



## Paternalism in the Textile Industry: Grievances without Unions

Just as Gastonia represents a distinct type of community and a peculiar religious situation, so do its textile mills represent a particular stage in the development of industry and industrial relations. In fact, the textile industry presents a paradox. On the one hand, it has been one of the most exploitative, alienating, and consistently "condemned" forms of industry. On the other hand, it has been among the slowest to witness union organization as a means of redressing grievances and moving toward industrial democracy. Precisely because of this unique status of the textile industry, it is important to show Gastonia's place in the process of industrialization. As we shall see, the answer to the peculiar lag in unionism lies largely in paternalism and there is some reason to think that the union activities in Gastonia would have failed regardless of the position of the church.

Before turning to Gastonia, it might help to consider some of the historical background of the textile industry. In the United States, the railroad was the burgeoning new industry of the latter half of the nineteenth century which focused popular attention and legislative action on monopoly, trusts, and industrial accidents. But a half century earlier in England, it was the textile industry which provoked alarm by introducing factory production on a wide scale. Engels and Marx were not alone offended.[37] Liberals and conservatives alike were con-

cerned by the rise of a new kind of working man, the industrial proletariat.38 Here was an employee not tied to a master as servant or slave and not integrated into any guild of peers, a man with little work skill whose only economic bargaining strength was the strength of numbers expressed in mob action, on the picket line, and at the ballot box. Likewise it was this technology that fathered the "Satanic mill" and its dark, diseased, crime-infested, godless, and seditious mill town. How to deal with this new kind of man, this new kind of town, was one of the prime social questions of the last century.

This question has been answered in two distinctly different ways. One involves the development of labor unions, the expansion of voting rights, the emergence of labor-oriented political parties, and universal public education: that is, the integration of the factory worker into middle-class society. The other answer, advanced by Robert Owen and other humanitarians, involves the isolation of the workers from corrupting influences under the benevolent guidance of the industrialists. This paternalism consists of paying low cash wages and providing a wide range of services to employees in order to control as much as necessary of their off-the-job life. In its most complete form, paternalism provides all the institutions that comprise the worker's community. In some cases the paternalist was required to supply such services in order to maintain an adequate labor force; often he has also been able to realize a considerable profit by charging exorbitant rent and food prices. Yet the essential element of the system is to provide an able and docile work force.

While paternalism is anachronistic in our current stage of industrialization, it was a progressive force 150 years ago. The English peasants thrown off the land by the Enclosure Acts were uneducated, poor, and unused to the ways of the urban industrial world. For them paternalism and the associated social welfare and religious revivalism provided a half-way



38\. Paul Mantoux, The Industrial Revolution in the Eighteenth Century (New York, Harper and Brothers, 1961); John Bowditch and Clement Ramsland, eds., Voices of the Industrial Revolution (Ann Arbor, University of Michigan Press, 1957); Arthur O. Lewis, Jr., ed., Of Men and Machines (New York, Dutton Co., 1963), especially Part 4, "The Machine as Enemy"; Bendix, op. cit.; and Smelser, op. cit.



house along the road from feudalism to modernity. Paternalism's regimentation provided a check against vice and chronic pauperism, which aided the workers' adaptation to the burgeoning industrial towns of England. Yet the adaptation was on management's terms.

Over the nineteenth century the English and American worker became more sophisticated, both in terms of general technical competence and in the ways of living in an urban context. The then most technologically advanced industries, such as the railroads, relied heavily on the more self-reliant workers. Here trade unions came to play an integral part in the training and discipline of workers.

At the same time, another group of industries, including textiles, continued to rely on paternalism and fought unionization as a threat to the paternal loyalty of the "child-workers." The textile industry had begun to flourish in New England. In order to maintain the pattern of paternalism, it recruited from the fresh waves of ignorant and low-skilled immigrants. Several decades after the Civil War, however, the industry began to move to the Piedmont region of the Southeast where it could rely on a steady stream of workers drawn by poverty from the small freeholdings of Appalachia. After the frustrations of subsistence farming, these workers were used to long hours and low wages; they were thankful for any employment.

Using such raw material, mill owners molded a kind of community which was no longer a half-way house but, as Pope's and other careful empirical studies have shown,[39] a closed community isolated from the rest of the world. Here the only

39. See particularly Richard C. Nyman, Union-Management Cooperation in the "Stretch-out" (New Haven, Yale University Press, 1934); Seymour L. Wolfbein, The Decline of a Cotton Textile City: A Study of New Bedford (New York, Columbia University Press, 1944); Herbert Lahne, The Cotton Mill Worker (New York, Farrar and Rinehart Inc., 1944); Harriet Herring, Passing of the Mill Village (Chapel Hill, University of North Carolina Press, 1949); Glenn Gilman, Industrial Relations in the Industrial Southeast: A Study of the Textile Industry (Chapel Hill, University of North Carolina Press, 1956); Solomon Barkin, "The Personality Profile of Southern Textile Workers," Labor Law Journal, 2 (1960), p. 5; John K. Morland, "Educational and Occupational Aspirations of Mill and Town School Children in a Southern Community," Social Forces, 39 (1960), p. 174; Robert Blauner, *Alienation and Freedom: The Factory Worker and His Industry* (Chicago, University of Chicago Press, 1964); Morland, op. cit. Industrial

economic opportunity was work at the mill, the “prison,” “sweatshop,” or “death hole,” as it was variously known. Even now, the work is simple, dull, and repetitious, though it demands constant attention. It often takes place in noisy, damp, lint-filled rooms, where the temperature sometimes reaches 100 degrees. The job routine does not allow for the development of work teams or for personal freedom and initiative. It allows little chance for the development of skills, let alone advancement up the industrial hierarchy. Yet despite these alienating conditions and despite the fact that the industry has been the target of social critics and trade unionists for more than a century and a half, it has been almost uniquely successful in repelling unionism. Even today less than 25 per cent of the textile industry is unionized.[40]

To the skimming reader, it may appear that Pope's answer to this paradox is to lay much of the blame on the churches. Yet even granting that the churches condoned the actions of the industrialists, never effectively spoke out against obvious abuses, and did not aid the union drive, it does not follow that if the churches had taken another position the outcome would have differed. Pope himself suggests that labor relations policies were established and maintained rather remotely from religious influences. Though the churches may have been the allies of management, it is probable that man-

relations vary widely from nation to nation as Arthur M. Ross and Paul T. Hartman, Changing Patterns of Industrial Conflict (New York, John Wiley, 1960), have shown. Industrial relations strategies also vary widely from industry to industry as shown in Clark Kerr and Abraham Siegal, "The Interindustry Propensity to Strike—an International Comparison," in Arthur Kornhauser et al., eds., Industrial Conflict (New York, McGraw-Hill, 1954), pp. 189-212. Yet in all this variation, the textile industry remains stubbornly resistant. Paternalistic management policies predominate in whatever country textiles are located, and there is a consistently high rate of industrial conflict as well. For specific studies of the textile industry in developing nations see: Frederick Harbison and I. Ibrahim, "Some Labor Problems of Industrialization in Egypt," Annals of the American Academy of the Political and Social Sciences (May, 1956); Manning Nash, Machine Age Maya: The Industrialization of a Guatemalan Community, American Anthropological Association, Memoirs 87 (Menasha, Wisconsin, 1958); Daniel Lerner, The Passing of Traditional Society: Modernizing the Middle East (New York, Free Press, 1958); and Richard D. Lambert, Workers, Factories, and Social Change in India (Princeton, Princeton University Press, 1963).

40. Blauner, op. cit., p. 62.

agement would have prevailed even without their support.

A number of factors suggest why paternalism has endured so long and why the union has been repelled so successfully. First, the textile industry is highly decentralized and competitive. There are many companies in the field; there is a chronic overcapacity; there is new competition from other products such as synthetic fibers and plastics. Wages comprise one of the few variable costs, and there is consequent pressure to keep them low and to recruit workers who will be satisfied with them.

Second, most jobs consist of tending machines, work which involves little skill and no formal schooling. This precludes the development of the skilled work force that Marx envisaged at the head of the proletariat and that did indeed lead the drive to unionism in, say, the printing industry.[41]

Third, insofar as any skilled jobs are available, strong sex segregation saves them for the men and relegates women to many of the more oppressive chores. Thus, the workers lack not only a large fund of skilled laborers for leadership but they also lack the ground swell of resentment from the unskilled ranks, since the women seem more apt to swallow their burden silently. This may be especially the case in traditional Southern society where women play more compelling roles in the family and treat their work as a perfunctory and secondary activity.

Fourth, the small mill community tends to be sufficiently integrative to heal many of the wounds incurred on the job. This is partly because of the lack of institutional differentiation. The close ties between extended family, church, neighborhood, and the economy foster Gemütlichkeit and a narrow, dependent perspective.

Fifth, paternalism itself is a counter to unionism. Management is directly implicated in the workers' lives. Until recently,

41. A searching analysis of unionization in the printing industry (as well as of the conditions for democracy in organizational structures generally) is *Union Democracy* by Seymour Martin Lipset, Martin A. Trow, and James S. Coleman (New York, Free Press, 1956). For a cogent comparative analysis of unionization in the printing, textile, auto, and chemical industries, see Blauner, op. cit.

42. See especially Herring, op. cit. But Herring does not point up the camouflaged irony here. Selling the textile worker the house he formerly rented makes him less and not more independent. Not only does mill management control the

the mills owned the workers' homes and were available for counsel on the workers' problems.[42] Under these conditions, union strikes would be more than a temporary suspension of wages. They could involve a loss of shelter and a termination of purchasing rights at the company store as well. Thus, going on strike could portend a far greater rupture in the accustomed style of life than would be the case for the Detroit automobile factory employee. It is also worth noting that because of these factors any strike that does occur is likely to have more serious consequences. In a traditional society unused to conflict, conflict once initiated is likely to become general since mechanisms for limiting it are not developed. This pattern can be seen in the two famous Kohler strikes in Wisconsin[43] as well as in the Loray strike in Gastonia.

In short, the textile workers are unlikely candidates for unionism despite their job conditions. The point is encapsulated in Morland's description of the textile worker's modal personality as of 1958:

The typical textile worker (1) accepts life as he finds it, and is not aggressive in seeking to change his condition. He is resigned to his lot. (2) He seems to be more noncompetitive than competitive, more dependent than independent, more submissive than dominant. (3) He lacks

financial structure by which ownership is obtained, but, once obtained, the worker is even more rooted in the village, even more obliged to continue work in its sole industry, and even more anxious to avoid strikes and other troubles that would stigmatize the community and decrease the value of his house. Docility becomes a mechanism for protecting his investment. And yet the reverse may also apply. A recent news story out of Alabama reports a textile worker who spoke out for civil rights but whom the mill managers could not afford to fire because of payments due them on the worker's house.

43. One of the most notable paternalistic managements of the last half century has been the Kohler family, manufacturers of bathroom fixtures and other porcelain ware, in Kohler, Wisconsin. Periods of tranquility in industrial relations have been punctuated by two long and bitter strikes. The "Kohler idea" and its consequences have never been subjected to thorough and nonpartisan analysis but there is one book dealing with the first strike, in 1934: Walter H. Uphoff, *The Kohler Strike* (Chicago, privately printed, 1935). Two works deal with aspects of the second strike, which lasted from 1954 until 1960: Sylver Petro, *The Kohler Strike* (Chicago, Regnery Press, 1961), and Saul Pett, "Kohler and the UAW: All that Remains is Hate," in Jack Barbash, ed., *Unions and Union Leadership* (New York, Harper, 1959), pp. 270-276.

confidence in himself and feels inadequate, especially when faced with new or difficult tasks. He is humble. (4) He is reserved, quiet, and cautious, particularly when with strangers. He seems to repress his feelings more than to give them expression. (5) He is suspicious of and even hostile toward outsiders, but he is loyal and devoted to his kindred group.[44]

Hardly a portrait of an industrial aggressor, such persons are not easily organized into an independent, ongoing labor union able to bargain responsibly with mill officials. Even the current increase in automation has so far produced little change in the situation.[45] As Robert Blauner has recently indicated, today's textile worker is remarkably akin to his predecessors of 1958, 1929, or even 1829.[46]

# Management vs Labor: White vs Negro

Although we have noted the similarity between the situation in Gastonia and the relations between religion, the economy, and the class structure in earlier eras, many of our subsequent comments have raised qualifications and limitations that the reader of Millhands and Preachers should bear in mind. Yet part of any true classic is that it ramifies beyond its immediate objectives. Here we want to take up one of the most important of these ramifications and show how the book can be extended rather than limited. Instead of reading it simply as an analysis of the role of religion in a purely economic struggle, it can be thought of as a case study of community crises generally. Instead of reading it solely for its analytic import, it can and should be treated for its insights into one of the most important substantive concerns of the "new South" and indeed the nation as a whole: civil rights.

44. Morland, op. cit., p. 251.

45. The prime consequence of the increasing use of automated technology in the textile industry has been reduced employment. There has been little increase in skill levels required. See Solomon Barkin, *The Decline of the Labor Movement* (Santa Barbara, Center for the Study of Democratic Institutions, 1961), and "Pollock Defeats Dissident Faction at Textile Workers Union Convention," *Business Week*, 1815 (June 13, 1964), 116.

46. Blauner, op. cit.

It is true that Pope chose not to study the Negroes on the grounds that they were irrelevant to the mill situation. Still, it is difficult to escape the similarities between the economic crisis of 1929 and the racial crisis of the sixties. Perhaps some of the following parallels will be featured in the planned restudy of Gastonia by Knudsen, Earle, and Shriver.

First, just as the churches were conscripted into the cause of management, they have frequently been foursquare in the cause of segregation. Campbell and Pettigrew's *Christians in Racial Crisis* presents findings highly reminiscent of Pope's. A study of the response of Protestant ministers to the Little Rock school desegregation crisis of 1957, the book concludes that the ministers were unable to advocate their privately asserted personal preference for integration for fear of losing their congregations. Some of the more fundamentalist ministers went so far as to justify segregation with a Biblical exegesis. The more sophisticated took advantage of a structurally differentiated metropolitan area to side-step the issue wherever possible. Unlike the Gastonia clergy, they were not forced to choose one side or the other. Instead they were allowed to use a number of rhetorical techniques to maintain a studied neutrality, which nevertheless lent support to segregation by default.

Second, the natural lines of interest-group cleavage were obscured and disturbed in both situations. In the strike of 1929 it was probably in the long-run interest of most of the Gastonia merchants and other townspeople to curb the power of the mill officials. Yet they supported the mill management in all of its actions in support of local, class, and religious issues. Likewise, more recently it has probably been in the interests of many whites to join with Negroes in opposing the "power structure." Yet the issue of race, like the past issue of localism, has served as a deflection.

There are further similarities between the Gastonia union organizers and the student volunteers who entered Mississippi in the summer of 1964. As a third parallel, just as the union organizers were met with cries of "foreigners" and "Communists," these were common charges in the speeches of Mississippi politicians concerning the students. Fourth, just as many of

the mill workers were reticent to aid the union for fear of reprisals, so were many of the Mississippi Negroes fearful of cooperating with the students because of threatened violence and economic recriminations. Fifth, just as the union leader, Beal, began by advocating nonviolence during the Loray strike and was only later pushed into violence by his less sophisticated rank-and-file followers, so was this the case with a number of Negro leaders who have had to sponsor limited violence to maintain their stature with the rank-and-file Negroes in strifetorn spots such as Harlem, as well as in Mississippi. Sixth, just as Gastonia reacted by barring parades and rallies, so did Mississippi enact new legislation increasing the penalty and reducing the legal justification for picketing and voter registration marches.

Seventh, just as law and order were suppressed at the time of the strike while law enforcement officials turned a participating hand and a blind eye toward acts of violence against the strikers, so have local Mississippi officials toward civil rights proponents. In both cases, there were flagrant miscarriages of justice. If there is any solace in the comparison, the injustices are now more confined to the Deep South; states like North Carolina have begun to move in more reasoned channels.

In all of this, then, Pope has provided much more than an analysis of one particular county in the midst of one particular crisis. Not only is his book a landmark in the study of religion and the economy in a community setting, but it should take its rank among the finest examinations of the traditional South and its reaction to threatened change. Like Cash's *The Mind of the South*, Myrdal's *An American Dilemma*, Dollard's *Caste and Class* in a Southern Town, and Davis and Gardners' *Deep South*, Pope's *Millhands and Preachers* affords penetrating insight into an area beset with perhaps the major social problem of our day. In a period that promises a surfeit of super-

48. W. J. Cash, The Mind of the South (New York, Alfred Knopf, 1941); Gunnar Myrdal, An American Dilemma: The Negro Problem and Modern Democracy (New York, Harper, 1944); John Dollard, Caste and Class in a Southern Town (New Haven, Yale University Press, 1937); Allison Davis, Burleigh B. Gardner, and Mary R. Gardner, Deep South (Chicago, University of Chicago Press, 1941). For a summary of research on the Southern community, see the excellent Chapter 7 in Stein, op. cit.

# INTRODUCTION

xlix

ficial treatments of the South and the racial crisis, it is a privilege to re-introduce a work that has endured for its penetration into the depths of the South in particular and social experience in general.

# POSTSCRIPT

UNFORTUNATELY, I am not personally acquainted with either Mr. Demerath or Mr. Peterson. Perhaps that is just as well; one could hardly expect to have a personal relationship with the pathologist who performs his post mortem examination. To contradict the metaphor, it is encouraging to learn that there is still life in Millhands and Preachers (that title, by the way, was chosen by the Yale Press; an impious colleague proposed at the time that the book should be entitled God and Gastonia).

Over the years I have watched successive printings of the book with interested detachment. This attitude has been prompted partly by removal from the locale of the study; I have not returned to Gastonia since the field work there was completed. Probably I could do so; one of the best reviews of the book appeared in the *Gastonia Gazette*, surmising that this was a book to end all books about Gaston County. Let the newspaper editor sharpen his pencil, as a restudy of his community by three brilliant young social scientists is currently under way.

It is good that they are young. Millhands and Preachers was a book that could be produced only by a young man. It required as much leg-work as brain-work. When I think of all the trudging, the dusty roads of Gaston County no longer beckon. But the questions and problems along those roads are as important as ever — perhaps more so. I am grateful to Messrs. Demerath and Peterson for putting them in perspective.

LISTON POPE

New Haven

October, 1964

# PART I

# PATTERNS OF GROWTH

![image](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2026-01-03/2a4bb434-3ed4-4ab2-bae3-79168a342ef6/236dbdec8811412cb1bde1f522e3291205b5f056d03f1b7dbcb5291631d03766.jpg)


# MILLHANDS AND PREACHERS

# CHAPTER I

# THE RISE OF THE COTTON MILLS

EARLY in 1929 the name of Gastonia was catapulted from obscurity into headlines around the world. In rapid succession metropolitan newspapers told their readers of a Communist uprising in the little textile town, of mass parades and the use of troops, of mob scenes and the destruction of life and property. Outbreaks of violence flared for several weeks, culminating in the death of the chief of police and the wounding of several of his officers in an armed skirmish with Communists and strikers. Scores of arrests followed, and two dramatic trials focused the spotlight of publicity on a tense courtroom for additional weeks. At last seven men were convicted of murder and sentenced to prison, only to escape their sentences by flight to Russia.

Readers across America were confirmed in many of their previous notions about the South, gleaned largely from novels and Hollywood, and were supplied with new items of information. Uneasy ghosts of the past were resurrected and stalked again through the public mind: young rowdies and enraged citizens riding the night in disguise; mobs bent on lynching; soft-spoken gentlemen ready to sudden violence; feudal barons who owned people body and soul and ruled in benevolent despotism. New specters of a New South were brought from concealment and placed on display before a national audience. It was revealed that many Southern children were nursed by spindles rather than corpulent Negro mammie; that "delicious Southern cooking" was not enough to forestall pellagra when there were only "fat back" and "corn pone" to cook; that the gentle mistress of the old plantation had a granddaughter who could be a hellcat on the picket line. Wages in Southern mills were depicted as low, hours long. Public officials were portrayed as hirelings of industry. Preachers were indicted as "moral police for the mill owners," and religious fundamentalism was denounced as prejudicial to legal justice. The traditional picture of a columnd mansion with magnolias in front and slave quarters behind was replaced by that of a low, oblong cotton mill with a picket line

in front and the identical houses of a mill village all about it. Gastonia became a symbol of the New South, and the connotations it carried have been remembered by many who have forgotten its name.

Most Southerners resented the unfavorable publicity attendant on the Gastonia episode and sought to dismiss it as another effort on the part of Northerners to disparage the South. They suspected that a disproportionate amount of attention was being given the affair: Communists had led strikes in other sections of the country, often with comparable violence, without bringing such notoriety to the scene of their operations. Proud of the phenomenal development of industry in their region within a few decades, the natives believed that the industrial strife and the deplorable conditions it revealed were merely temporary aberrations, and that a distorted picture was being presented to the nation as typical. A few Southerners were inclined to announce the end of an economic era, but most of their fellow citizens saw no portent in Gastonia and explained their own excitement (many newspapers called it "hysteria") as due only to the disruption of their usual peace.

The wider American audience was correct in assuming that Gastonia and all its symbolized posed problems of national interest and concern. Southerners were correct in believing that their New South could be understood only by careful appraisal of events more far-reaching than those which transpired in Gastonia in 1929. Back of the violence and headlines lay a series of developments extending over several decades, and some of them had been almost as revolutionary in character as the eruption which focused national attention on their results. For the understanding of those developments and for some insight into the operation of religious institutions in a changing society, the county in which Gastonia is located is a prize laboratory.

# THEBACKGROUND OF THE RISE OF THE MILLS

The story of the rise of the cotton mills in the South has been often told. Sometimes it has been told in the mood of a romance, portraying the Herculean efforts of a region, defeated and impoverished in war, to lift itself again from poverty and to regain a respectable place in the national economy through tak

ing upon itself an industrial character formerly despised. Again, the industrial revolution ushered in by the rise of the mills has been depicted as the great betrayal of a culture which, though temporarily broken by war, might have been revived, preserving greater beauty and humanity than its betrayer has been able to bring. Still again, the rise of young industrial giants, unshackled and insolent in power, has been denounced as a farce, a burlesque of a development which, under other conditions or leadership, might have been genuinely beneficial. Onc mood or other—romance, tragedy, or farce—has generally prevailed in the talling of the tale.

Numerous attempts have also been made to study dispassionately the rise of the mills, and have succeeded in the isolation of a number of factors underlying their emergence. The part played by religious institutions has been largely ignored, despite the fact that it was of considerable importance. In order to see it in proper perspective, however, it is necessary briefly to mention the associated factors, more secular in character, which figured in the complex background of industrial transformation. Gaston County affords an excellent context in which to study the relative importance of the various transforming forces—forces which, in a period of only three or four decades, brought profound changes in the economic life of the entire South and of the Piedmont section in particular. New impulses stirring in the region were less restrained in this county than in most areas, and arc therefore more easily studied. The further fact that economic transformation was effected there almost entirely by local leadership and local capital renders close analysis doubly instructive, as contrasted with counties where industrialism was imported.

Prior to 1880 Gaston County was mainly agricultural in economic character. Its agriculture differed in crucial respects, however, from that typical of the Southern plantation economy. Lying at the edge of a mountainous region, its topography was better suited to small farms than to extensive plantations, and that type of economic organization had prevailed from the time of its settlement. Few residents before the Civil War had owned more than three or four slaves; the percentage of slaves in the total population of Lincoln County (including Gaston) in 1810 was 15.2 as compared with an average of over

45 per cent in several eastern counties of North Carolina in which the plantation system ruled.1 Farms in the county in 1880 averaged about 130 acres in size, with a very small minority being larger than a thousand acres.2 Cotton culture accounted for only 18.38 per cent of all the acres under cultivation in that year; more than twice as much acreage was devoted to corn.3 The agricultural life of the county differed greatly, therefore, from the cotton-slavery-plantation system which constituted the basic economic structure of the South. A similar economic pattern extended over many other counties in the Piedmont region and comprised a sort of marginal economy in the Southern scene.

Professor Broadus Mitchell has pointed out that manufacturing had little opportunity to rise in the Southern states during the decades immediately preceding the Civil War.4 Occasional prophets of industrialism appeared, but they were voices crying in vain against the slavery-cotton-plantation system. Only in counties in which this system was largely absent did independent manufacturing enterprises, as contrasted with small domestic crafts on the plantations, appear to any extent. Gaston County was one of these. At the beginning of the nineteenth century the iron industry reached considerable development there.5 The tradition of cotton manufacturing was also established in the vicinity before the Civil War. The first cotton mill south of the Potomac was erected, about 1816, on the South Fork River just north of the present boundary line of Gaston County, part of the machinery being made by skilled ironwork

5. Hugh T. Lefler, ed., North Carolina History Told by Contemporaries (Chapel Hill, 1934), pp. 26, 102-103, 118-122, 226, containing reprints of documents of the period which described the iron industry in the county.

ers in the community and the remainder imported from New England.6 A second branch of this mill was erected in 1819 and was operated very profitably for several decades. The first mills located within the present boundaries of the county appeared about 1850 when a mill was moved there from another county in order to obtain water power and two mills were built by local promoters. Further development was impeded by the depletion of capital during the Civil War. Three cotton factories continued to operate, however, and the rapid emergence of new mills after 1880 represented a continuation of a previous tendency rather than a completely new departure.7 Several men who subsequently became pioneers in the erection of mills in the county received industrial training in these ante bellum mills.

The rapid extension of the textile industry in the county after 1880 was facilitated by numerous factors, often analyzed. Natural advantages included an ample supply of water

7. A sharp debate has occurred as to whether the Southern industrialism beginning about 1880 was a new development or an outgrowth and extension of a trend established before the Civil War and merely interrupted temporarily by that war. Professor Broadus Mitchell, op. cit., has been the chief exponent of the former view. The latter view has been presented by Edgar Gardner Murphy, *Problems of the Present South* (New York, 1905); Richard H. Edmonds, *Facts about the South* (Baltimore, 1894); Goebel, op. cit., chap. iv; and many others. Gaston County contributes evidence in favor of the latter view. Professor Mitchell's thesis draws too sharply the line between the Old and New South. His argument that the plantation system was antithetical to independent industrialism is virtually unchallenged, but it is not to be assumed that the plantation system, however dominant, was the sole type of Southern economic enterprise or that its exclusive sway in certain regions prevented an almost unbroken line of industrial development in other sections, and notably in those in which postwar industrialism flourished. Industrial enterprise is relatively insignificant to this day in counties in which the plantation system was characteristic; it was of some importance, even before the Civil War, in other counties, especially in the Piedmont region. In these latter counties, including Gaston, the war brought an interlude, not a break.

8. Ben F. Lemert, The Cotton Textile Industry of the Southern Appalachian Piedmont (Chapel Hill, 1933), chaps. i-iii; C. K. Brown, "Industrial Development in North Carolina," The Annals of the American Academy of Political and Social Science, CLIII, 133-140 (January, 1931); Jefferson Bynum, "Pied

power, available from the Catawba River at the eastern boundary of the county, the South Fork River traversing the county diagonally, and a number of smaller streams. All the earlier mills were erected along the banks of these rivers, and subsequent development of cheap hydroelectric power from these streams made further industrial expansion especially easy. Natural advantages included also a climate suitable for cotton spinning, though in some respects not so favorable as that of New England.

Several economic factors were conducive to further development of industry. As already indicated, organization of the economic life of the county by small, atomistic units rendered transition to an industrial economy much easier than in counties in which traditions of a plantation economy lingered. Postwar economic developments stimulated the transition. In the 1870's the price of cotton dropped severely, and for nearly forty years thereafter seldom rose above 10 cents a pound.[9] The ante bellum advantage in producing cotton rather than in processing it was thereby reversed, and cotton mills sprang up in the cotton fields to share with Northern mills the profits accruing from fabrication.[10]

The construction of railroads hastened industrialization. Before the Civil War, the rise of manufacturing had been severely hampered by lack of efficient transportation facilities. One railroad touched Gaston County, but it had only one station there. In 1872 the Southern Railway came through; another railroad crossed it in 1875, with the present site of the city of Gastonia, then open country, as the point of intersection.[11] New markets

The roads went through a cow pasture to the south of Dallas, and Gastonia grew up, eventually replacing Dallas as county seat and principal town.

and supplies were thereby brought within easy reach. Electric power lines soon followed the courses of the railroads, and mills subsequently were erected beside railroads rather than rivers.

Human resources were also of crucial importance. The original colonizers of the county were chiefly Seoteh Irish and Germans, moving into the Piedmont and western sections of North Carolina about the middle of the eighteenth century because of increasing land rents and other economic difficulties in Pennsylvania; and Seotch Highlanders, moving westward from the eastern eounties of North Carolina as the development of plantations increasingly foreed the yeoman farmer off the more fertile land.[12] All three of these groups of settlers had traditions of craftsmanship, and the attention of their descendants was easily directed to industry when the prospect of economic advantage appeared. The economic condition of the population after the Civil War was of more importance, however, than its ethnic backgrouns. The poverty of small farmers and tenant farmers provided a great reservoir of cheap labor for any enterprise that promised a decent livelihood. As the price of cotton declined, opportunity for employment at each wage became increasingly attractive. Thousands of unsuccessful farmers, able to eke out only a mean livelihood in agriculture,[13] stood ready to furnish man power for new industrial enterprises.[14] Between 1880 and 1905 several thousand of them came to the new mills in Gaston County from contiguous rural eounties. A survey of 100 families of mill workers in the county in 1914 revealed that 73 had come from eounties immediately bordering Gaston; 66 had been tenants, not owning land.[15] Beginning

About 1870 the widespread introduction of the ring spindle, largely automatic in operation, rendered lack of industrial training fairly inconsequential, and facilitated the transition of workers from farms to cotton mills. Most of the Southern mills, and especially those in Gaston County, specialized at the outset in the production of coarser yarns, mostly 4's to 12's, which required less skill on the part of labor than other textile processes. Finer goods continued to be produced almost exclusively by New England mills.

Only four of the seventy-three families had lived at the mills all their lives; the median time of residence in a mill village was

about 1905, recruits were drawn increasingly from the mountainous regions farther to the west in North Carolina, Georgia, and Tennessee; the growth of towns in Gaston County had increased the prices of agricultural products sufficiently to allow profitable operation of small farms, and most of the surplus agricultural labor in the immediate vicinity had been already absorbed. Labor agents were sent by manufacturers to distant counties and even into adjoining states; the number of mills was growing so rapidly that an adequate supply of labor was beginning to constitute a problem, and the practice of "stealing labor" had become so prevalent that employers were forced to declare a truce and to seek new recruits. Additional thousands of dispirited farmers and mountaineers accepted the offer of the mill companies to move them and their possessions to the new mill villages.[16]

This labor supply was not drawn, as often supposed, from the class usually called "poor whites." Specifically, "poor whites" in ante bellum Southern society were to be found principally in plantation regions—that is, in the eastern coastal counties—where they comprised a marginal, pauperized, and often diseased group.[17] The mill workers in Gaston County were drawn chiefly from the descendants of small independent farmers in the Piedmont and mountainous areas who had fallen on evil days and lost their land.

The impoverished whites represented, in many respects, an ideal labor supply for the development of industry in the region. They were almost completely homogeneous in race and

Arnett, The South Looks at Its Past (Chapel Hill, 1935), pp. 47-50, 127. “Po’ white trash” supplied labor for occasional mills in the eastern counties of North Carolina (E. R. language, $^{18}$  and no special problems were involved in housing them in the mill villages or in the management of them by the mill executives. The latter, indeed, were of precisely the same racial and cultural background and knew how to handle their employees with adeftness bred of long association. The workers brought with them to the mill villages an individualism nurtured by solitary life on small farms and sparsely populated mountainsides; this individualism resulted, in the sphere of industrial relations, in personal dealing as individuals with the employer, with collective labor action appearing only sporadically and always being short-lived. The early manufacturers, lacking capital and depending heavily on uninterrupted production in order to remain solvent, had available a labor supply largely unamenable to outside influences interested in the organization of labor.

The labor supply was notable also for its docility. This trait has been overemphasized by chambers of commerce and power companies seeking to attract Northern capital to Southern communities, and has faded into the background of promotional literature since the wave of Southern strikes in 1929. The first generation of workers appears to have been, nevertheless, patient and long-suffering in spirit. Its members came to the mill villages as refugees; for the most part, they had been unsuccessful in previous economic pursuits, and acceptance of industrial employment represented a last stand against pauperism. They had been accustomed to working from sunrise to sunset without murmuring, and to expect little in return. They took it for granted that all members of the family would work as early as possible, and offered practically no opposition to child labor in the cotton mills. They begat notoriously large families, even surpassing the immigrants who populated the East with a plentiful supply of workers.[19]

Of the factors favoring the rise of industry in the South,

18. In 1930 only 212 foreign-born whites lived in Gaston County as compared with 65,489 native-born.

19. It is a commonplace that the birth rate in the South exceeds that of any other region in the nation. North Carolina has generally had a higher birth rate than any other state (G. T. Schwenning, "Prospects of Southern Textile Unionism," The Journal of Political Economy, XXXIX, 789-790 [December, 1931]). According to the 1940 Census, North Carolina led the nation in the size of its families, with an average of 4.5 persons per family; the average for the South Atlantic states was 4.2 and for the nation 3.8.

perhaps the availability of an almost inexhaustible supply of cheap, tractable labor was the most important. Competition for jobs was so severe that wages were minimal, with the manufacturer furnishing housing and many other facilities for the maintenance of life. Additional thousands of tenant farmers and mountaineers stood ready to take the jobs of mill workers who, through strikes or other forms of noncooperation, might seem to the employers and to Southern communities to have forfeited their right to employment. Under such conditions employers had a high competitive advantage over Northern mills at a time when lack of capital and experience made an advantage especially necessary. The same factors have preserved traditional industrial relations almost unchanged down to the present, though the argument from necessity has become increasingly less convincing.

Negroes, comprising a relatively small percentage of the population of the county and its surrounding territory,[20] were excluded from the mills almost entirely. The argument that this was because they were less efficient than whites is of dubious validity; several ante bellum Southern mills, including two in Gaston County, had been operated solely by slaves, with satisfactory results.[21] After emancipation, however, the status of the Negro was changed from that of a valuable economic asset to that of the poor white man's last outpost against oblivion—an oblivion to which he had previously been consigned because of his inability to compete with slave labor. Negroes were excluded, therefore, from the cotton mills, or, in the case of the few who were admitted, given the most disagreeable jobs. Their extensive employment would have endangered the availability of an adequate supply of white labor, after the bitterness generated by reconstruction tactics. The rise of the mills represented to considerable degree the economic triumph of the poor

20. Twenty-eight per cent in Gaston County in 1880, and an even smaller percentage in many of the counties lying west of Gaston. The county contained 12,392 Negroes in 1930 as compared with 65,701 whites.

0-1-21 Goebel, op. cit., pp. 150-151; Sterling D. Spero and Abram L. Harris, The Black Worker: The Negro and the Labor Movement (New York, 1931), pp. 348-349; Robert F. Cope, "History of Early Cotton Mills in Gaston County," Gastonia Gazette, November 20, 1934. Whites and blacks were employed alongside each other in a few of the ante bellum Southern mills, without difficulty.

white man over the emancipated black.[22] As the whites moved into the cotton mills, the Negroes drifted rapidly into farm tenancy and to Northern cities.

Cultural changes, less tangible but no less significant than the factors enumerated above, also influenced the appearance of cotton mills in the southern Piedmont region. In many communities, and especially in counties where the plantation system had not prevailed, a sweeping change in ethicways[23] occurred during the two decades immediately after the Civil War. In economic philosophy, agrarianism was modified by a new emphasis on industrialism; in political affairs sectionalism was increasingly qualified by a spirit of nationalism. An earlier Southern liberalism, associated especially with tobacco planters of the eighteenth century, had been vanquished by provincial ideas as the interests of the South became identified with the perpetuation of slavery and reliance upon cotton.[24] The provincial creeds failed to vindicate themselves on the battlefield, however, and the way was paved for a re-emergence of cosmopolitan viewpoints. In the 1880's, "nationalism and industrialism" had become the creed of liberal spirits in the South.[25] Many communities set out to compete with the North in industrial enterprise, and thereby to wrest victory from defeat.

The fact that Southern communities deliberately determined to build cotton mills is of major importance both in tracing the rise of the mills themselves and in studying subsequent industrial relations in the industry. Towns were willing to make almost any concession in order to obtain mills: taxes were kept low $^{26}$  and often rebated, restrictive legislation was carefully

23. This term is intended to indicate common social judgments as to what ought to prevail in society, whether it does or not, in contradistinction to "mores," which pertains especially to judgments that the folkways actually followed are the ones that ought to be followed for the welfare of society.

24. A penetrating analysis of this shift in political and economic philosophy is contained in Clement Eaton, Freedom of Thought in the Old South (Durham, N.C., 1940).

 26. The tax rate in Gastonia has always been low: 20 c. per $100 in 1887,

avoided, natural and human resources of the communities were offered at nominal prices, and industrialists were accepted as pioneers and unchallenged arbiters of social and moral welfare. Villages vied with each other in efforts at industrialization; the number of cotton mills in the vicinity became an infallible index to the "progressive spirit" of any "thriving, growing city." Gastonia boasted in 1906: "There are more cotton factories in Gaston County than in any other county in the South, and there are more of these factories in Gastonia than in any other town in the County."[27] Brochures depicting tiny Southern villages as industrial Edens were prepared and distributed widely.[28] A contagion for building cotton mills swept over the entire Piedmont region—one observer remarked that it was "like the measles."[29]

Without community enthusiasm of this sort it is probable that the establishment of mills in the South would have been deferred for a few decades, or that their erection would have occurred under different auspices. Despite a prevalent notion to the contrary, most of the Southern mills were not built by Northern capital; a major portion of them were indigenous projects, financed by a great number of local citizens. In the 1880's, an adequate supply of capital could be obtained locally only through widespread community participation. The richest man in Gaston County prior to the Civil War had about a thousand dollars available for investment. The Gastonia Town Tax Book for 1887 listed the property of no citizen at more than \(10,000.[30] It was necessary, therefore, that capital should be supplied by numerous small investors. Small merchants became, in several instances, focal points around which capital might be organized. When a stock-subscription campaign for a new mill

For example, an illustrated booklet, *High Shoals*, *Gaston County*, *North Carolina*, published in 1908, contains an effusive picture of the opportunities awaiting industry there, including pleasures that tired industrialists might have from fishing in the river. “The wheels of the factory,” says the booklet, “have been so choked with eels that it was necessary to stop and chop them out of the buckets.” The village had only a few hundred inhabitants.

was announced, dozens of would-be stockholders responded, often calling on banks for funds, with small items of real property as surety. Shares frequently were sold on the installment plan, with payments as low as one dollar a month. The services of investment bankers were seldom solicited; an advertisement in the local newspaper was generally a successful substitute. Textile machinery dealers and building contractors cooperated, sometimes accepting stock in lieu of cash payment for machinery and construction costs. Nearly all the managers of the mills were natives of the county,[31] chosen because of their ability at promotion or experience in textile operation rather than because of majority stock ownership.

Up to 1910 the mills in Gaston County were owned, to large degree, by small investors—farmers, merchants, clerks, wage earners, ministers. Subsequently, the relative number of stockholders declined as successful manufacturers gathered stock increasingly into their own hands and furnished most of the capital for new mills. Few public stock issues have been announced since 1921.[32] But traditions of local ownership and community sponsorship of the mills have continued. Citizens still aver that their industry is owned and operated almost entirely by natives. As a matter of fact, this conception is only partly true; of 114 persons holding major offices in the mill corporations of the county in 1935, 52 were not natives of the county and 16 were nonresidents.[33] Notions of community interest in the mills and community responsibility for their success have survived, however, and have affected most efforts to regulate industrial practices. The county has not recognized that it has increasingly been sheltering absentee enterprise; it has continued to think of its mills in affectionate, paternal terms.
